(* -*- mode:text; mode:outline-minor;  -*- *)

mwdoc.txt
begun 12-03-2003
Utilize the Monier-Williams dictionary of the Cologne Digital Sanskrit Project.

* mwtot2.txt
  This is the original form of the file, from the down loaded 'mwsdd' program
of Bontes, which "is an interface to the Monier Williams Sanskrit dictionary, that has been digitised by the university of koln". 
mwsdd:  http://members.chello.nl/l.bontes/sans_n.htm
 For the current version and the project report on the Cologne Sanskrit
 Dictionary Project (CDSL), see
koln: http://webapps.uni-koeln.de/tamil/
      http://www.uni-koeln.de/phil-fak/indologie/tamil/mwreport.html

* Construct SLmwtot2.txt from mwtot2.txt
** (construct-SLmwtot2)
 ; constructs SLmwtot2.txt from mwtot2.txt. 
 ; it deletes a few characters believed to be spurious, and
 ; it changes to printable characters two meaningful but
 ; unprintable characters.
  a. The following character codes are deleted from the file: 
     ASCII COUNT
hat-M   13 2  (spurious)
hat-U   21 1  (unidentified)
<    60 1  (spurious)
>    62 2  (spurious)
^    94 47  (spurious)
Å 129 6  (unidentified)
Ñ 132 3  (unidentified)
â 137 5  (unidentified)
î 148 4  (unidentified)
???  179 1  (unidentified)
???  205 1  (unidentified)
???  254 28  (unidentified)

  b. The following unprintable character codes are changed
     to printable character codes not appearing in the original file:
???  241 15003  ('circled English abbreviated words expanded') -> [d'035]='#'
???  243 7097   ('circled Sanskrit abbreviated words expanded')-> [d'124]='|'

* CHANGE LOG

** (modify-SLmwtot2-1a) - examine for grouping errors.
 Checks SLmwtot2.txt for grouping errors within records,
 where grouping is by (), [], {}, and `" "'.
 There were 2177 such records noted.
 These were written out to file "modify-SLmwtot2-1a.txt".
 In addition to noting the record, the program writes out
 a changed record, where the errors are replaced with
 strings of the form <ERR=REASON>. 
 I originally thought to correct the errors manually. 
 The current method of automatic 'correction' essentially
 defers a 'correct' correction until some later time; ideally,
 the authors of the data would incorporate the changes at that
 stage. Nonetheless, the initial effort at manual correction
 may proved useful later, and I have kept the results in the
 file "modify-SLmwtot2-1-prepa.txt".
** "modify-SLmwtot2-1-prepa.txt" (FYI)
 This file contains some corrections manually made, based
 on an earlier version of the grouping error detection program.
 In this process, I tried to make meaningful corrections to the
 grouping errors. This was carried out through about
 line 21647. Several other kind of errors were noted.
 Note that these changes have NOT been incorporated,
 but may be appropriate to incorporate later.
*** lines 2227-2930
Based on MW p. 10, line 2928 is changed to
 mfn. unknown , (gana %{yAvA7di} q.v.)
For the time being, line 2929 and 2930 are changed to duplicate
lines 2927 and 2928.

*** Lines 2929-2930.
 These should be deleted.
These show as
ajJAtaka
 mfn. unknown , (g.
yAvA7di
 q.v.) 
Lines 2929 and 2930  should be deleted. 

*** Lines 21627 - 21647:
 In all of these, 'a-yatha' should be 'a-yathA' (MW p.84)
** (modify-SLmwtot2-1a-process)
 Changes the records of SLmwtot2 according to the
  'NEW' records of file 'modify-SLmwtot2-1a.txt'

** (modify-SLmwtot2-1b) - examine for transliteration errors
 Checks SLmwtot2.txt for transliteration errors.
 There were 203 such records noted.
 These were written out to file "modify-SLmwtot2-1b.txt".
 In addition to noting the record, the program writes out
 a changed record, where the errors are replaced with
 strings of the form <ERR=REASON>. 

 Here is a description of the process:
  Check each data line for valid characters within embedded Sanskrit,
  i.e., the characters between braces in expressions like '%{a-tIkSNa4}'.
  A similar check is made for the 'key' lines, which contain the
  key similarly encoded, but with the enclosing pattern '%{}'.
  The compound-separation character '-' is retained.
  The Sanskrit-word-abbreviation character '|' is ignored. (about 3500)
  The English-word-abbreviation character '#' is considered an error.
  For records with a decoding problem, a 4-line error message is written:
 OLD <line#> 
 <erroneous line>
 NEW <line#> 
 <'corrected' line>
 These lines are written to file 'modify-SLmwtot2-1b.txt', or
 to file 'tabout' in directory 'MW' if that optional function argument
 is present.
** (modify-SLmwtot2-1b-process)
 Changes the records of SLmwtot2 according to the
  'NEW' records of file 'modify-SLmwtot2-1b.txt'

** (modify-SLmwtot2-1c) - examine 1st char in data lines
 Checks SLmwtot2.txt for validity of 1st character in data line.
 This should be either blank (the norm) or a digit, indicating
 an alternate derivation.
 Note that blank lines are also invalid.
 There were 892 such records noted.
 These were written out to file "modify-SLmwtot2-1c.txt".
 In addition to noting the record, the program writes out
 a changed record, where the errors are replaced with
 strings of the form <ERR=REASON>. 

 OLD <line#> 
 <erroneous line>
 NEW <line#> 
 <'corrected' line>
 The corrected line has form
  -" <ERR=FCc>...rest of line...", if c is 1st char and is not '<' or '\n'
  -" <ERR=EMPTY>", if the line is empty (c='\n'). This is is usual error
  -" <ERR=FCLP>...rest of line...", if c is '<'. NOTE: These entered the
   file as <ERR=LP>, (first character an erroneous grouping character).
** (modify-SLmwtot2-1c-process)
 Changes the records of SLmwtot2 according to the
  'NEW' records of file 'modify-SLmwtot2-1c.txt'

** (modify-SLmwtot2-1d) -  keys ending in '-' -> <ERR=BADKEY>
 Checks SLmwtot2.txt for validity of last character in key line.
 This should not be the 'compound' character '-'.
 There were 46 key records ending in '-'.
 These were written out to file "modify-SLmwtot2-1d.txt".
 In addition to noting the record, the program writes out
 a changed record, where the errors are replaced with
 strings of the form <ERR=REASON>. 

 OLD <line#> 
 <erroneous line>
 NEW <line#> 
 <'corrected' line>
 The corrected line has form
  'ERR=BADKEY:key>', where 'key' is the rest of the line.
** (modify-SLmwtot2-1d-process)
 Changes the records of SLmwtot2 according to the
  'NEW' records of file 'modify-SLmwtot2-1d.txt'

** (modify-SLmwtot2-1e) - data with semicolon embedded in group
 In MW, the semi-colon's major function is to provide major separate
 senses of a definition. However, sometimes the semi-colon appears within
 grouping characters (e.g., within parenthetical comments).  To simplify
 the further identification of the major definitional components, what I
 may call the definitional clauses, it would be useful to replace the
 semicolons embedded in grouping elements with some-other character(s);
 I have chosen to use the string <ERR=SC>.
 There were 5647 such instances noted.
 These were written out to file "modify-SLmwtot2-1e.txt".
 In addition to noting the record, the program writes out
 a changed record, where erroneous semicolons are replaced by <ERR=SC>.

 OLD <line#> 
 <erroneous line>
 NEW <line#> 
 <'corrected' line>
** (modify-SLmwtot2-1e-process)
 Changes the records of SLmwtot2 according to the
  'NEW' records of file 'modify-SLmwtot2-1e.txt'

** (modify-SLmwtot2-misc)
 This reads and processes corrections from files modify-misc1.txt, etc.


** (construct-SLmwtot3) 
 ; translates %{..} to SLP transliteration.
 Input file is SLmwtot2.txt. Output file is SLmwtot3.txt.
 Note that SLmwtot2.txt has only various corrections, using
 the traceable <ERR...> form. By contrast, there is lost information
 in SLmwtot3, namely, the accent information of the Sanskrit words
 and embedded text.
  This conversion modifies mwtot2.txt as follows:
  1. Each 'key' line is translated from HK form to SLP1 form, keeping
     both '-' as compound separators and '@' as word separators.
     The '|' character (Sanskrit-word-completion) is ignored.
  2. Within each 'data' line, embedded sanskrit (indicated by %{...})
     is translated similarly.



** (modify-SLmwtot3-1a) -  "N." preceded by non-blank
 In MW, the two-characters "N." are used as an abbreviation for 
  "name (also equal title or epithet)". 
 This appears 40415 times.
 Of these, 'N. of '  occurs 40142 times.

 One would normally expect a space or punctuation character to precede
 "N.". Of the 5492 instances of a non-space character preceding "N.",
 3838 instances are the single-quote which ends the two-character sequence 
 (double-quote singlequote "') used to terminate quotations in MW;
 In this case, the string should probably be "' , N." instead of "'N.".

** (modify-SLmwtot3-1a-process)
 Changes the records of SLmwtot3 using regexp-replace.
 Changes "'N." to "' , N."

** (modify-SLmwtot3-1b) -  "N." preceded by non-blank
 After having done (modify-SLmwtot3-1a-process), there are
 1654 instances of "xN." where 'x' is non-blank.

** (modify-SLmwtot3-1b-process)
 Changes the records of SLmwtot3 using regexp-replace.
 Changes "xN." to "x N.", where 'x' is any non-blank.

** (modify-SLmwtot3-2) ; add codes to key line
 Add information to the 'key' lines of SLmwtot3.
 This is in preparation for (construct-SLmwtot4)

<MWL=record-number>
 The record-number (line-number) of the original MW key record for
 the data definition (an odd number), 1 to 332867.
<DER=derivation-num> the derivation number implied by the
 first character of the definitional record in MW. 
 When this first character is a space, the derivation number 
 is implicitly a 1; all but 4604 records are of this form.
 This element is omitted in this predominant case.
<SKIP> if the record is not meaningful for some reason.
   The reasons are:
   1. <ERR=BADKEY...> on the key line
   2. <ERR=EMPTY> on the data line

codes with a more standard form, <LEX=...>. 
The changes are based on the results as shown in the
 'lexical categories' entry below.
All the various 'nominal' changes are made here.
The many lower-frequency changes are made first

** (modify-SLmwtot3-4a) some LEX changed to ELX
 There are some instances where a lexical code is embedded
 in one of the grouping elements ((),[],{},`" "'). These instances
 will need special attention, and are marked as ELX for the time being
(modify-SLmwtot3-4a (SLtot3-buffer))
# records  = 332869
#  changes  = 5815

** (modify-SLmwtot3-4b1) remove some lines ending in "; *$"
There are some lines ending in a semicolon with spaces.
These are faulty clauses, and are removed here in prepartion
for clause recognition.
(modify-SLmwtot3-4b1)
12 changes: ; $ -> 

** (modify-SLmwtot3-4b) some LEX changed to LX1
 There are some instances where a more than 1 LEX code appears
 in a given clause (defined by semicolons). 
 These instances will need special attention, and are marked as 
 LX1 for the time being.
(modify-SLmwtot3-4b (SLtot3-buffer))
# records  = 332869
#  changes  = 4716

** (modify-SLmwtot3-4b-analyze): analysis of LX1
In the course of constructing 'mw-verb.txt', I noticed that the
important verb 'gam' was absent. This was because the it had only
'LX1' codes: many different forms were put in before the definition
proper began.  In this case, anyway, the LX1 code could have
remained an LEX code: there was no real reason to change to LX1.
This prompted an analysis of LX1 codes, to see what's there.
Almost all records with an LX1=V occur only with other LX1=V's,
as with 'gam'. In a few cases, LX1=V occurs with another category,
e.g. with a nominal or indeclineable category; certainly, these
remain questionable.
*** results of analysis
(modify-SLmwtot3-4b-analyze)

** (modify-SLmwtot3-4c) some LEX changed to LX2
 There are some instances where a  LEX code appears
 in a given clause, but there is no definitional information
 in the clause. By 'no definitional information', I mean 
 that the rest of the clause is composed of blanks.
 These instances will need special attention, and are marked as 
 LX2 for the time being.
(modify-SLmwtot3-4c (SLtot3-buffer))
#  changes  = 2769


** (modify-SLmwtot3-4c1) some semi-colon corrections
Except for 4 instances, semi-colons appear only between spaces;
these are changed.
(regexp-distrib (SLtot3-buffer) ".;.")
ncount = 119442
' ; '  119438
' ;%'  3
'k; '  1
(modify-SLmwtot3-4c1)
1 changes: k; -> k ;
3 changes: ;% -> ; %

** (modify-SLmwtot3-5a) Some semicolons changed to "OR"
Sometimes the semicolon represents a different sense but no difference
in lexical category; in other cases the different sense is accompanied
by a different lexical category.
Here, we want to change a semicolon to the (not elsewhere occurring)
string "OR" when no difference in lexical category is intended.
NOTE: For verbs, this will need further modification later, for instance:

aMS <MWL=17>
 <LEX=V 10 P> %{aMSayati} , to divide , distribute L. OR also occasionally A1. %{aMSayate} L. OR also %{aMSApayati} L. 

In this instance, the 2nd clause ' also occasionally A1. %{aMSayate} L.'
 actually indicates another lexical category (eg., <LEX=V 10 A>). 
(modify-SLmwtot3-5a)
# records  = 332869
#  changes  = 68323


** NOTE on <LEX> at this point
There are currently 166434 initial clauses and 51119 non-initial clauses,
for a total of 217553 clauses. 
Currently, there are 177226 lexical codes; since there is at most one
lexical code per clause, 81% of the clauses have lexical specifications.
This still leaves 40327 clauses which either have no lexical 
specification (it is believed this is a small, but non-zero number),
or a lexical specification whose encoding is not yet understood.

Of the 177226 lexical codes, 
 140441 (79%) are in the initial clause of a definition
  36785 (21%) are in a non-initial clause of a definition

Of the 140441 lexical codes in initial clauses,
 129826 (92%) are preceded in the clause by nothing else, while
  10615 (8%) have some prelexical information, of many varieties.

Of the 36785 lexical codes in non-initial clauses,
 12495 (34%) are preceded by nothing else in the clause, while
 24290 (66%) have some prelexical information, of many varieties.

It seems that (+ 129826 12495) = 143321 (80%) of the 177226 lexical codes
occur in a clause with no preceding information.  These are likely
to be ready for use.

Over 96% (170523) of the 177226 lexical codes are nominal.

A clause at this time begins in one of two ways:
 "^ " a line beginning with blank.
      The key lines begin with a letter except for two cases:
      -tta and -tti. I am not sure how to interpret these entries;
      In SLmwtot3-modify-5a1, I change the lex codes to <LX3=...>
 "; " a semicolon. All semicolons are followed by a blank.
(regexp-distrib (SLtot3-buffer) "; " )
ncount = 51119
';'  51119
t
(regexp-distrib (SLtot3-buffer) "^ " )
ncount = 166434
' '  166434
t


(regexp-distrib (SLtot3-buffer) "<LEX=." )
ncount = 177226

'<LEX=V'  1882

** (modify-SLmwtot3-5a2) <PREVERB> added to some key records
 Some roots (notably 'i') have prefixed forms whose entries
 obscure the presence of a prefix by having no '-'.
 For these entries, the key record has a field <PREVERB>
 simplying flagging it as a verb which is prefixed.

** (modify-SLmwtot3-6a1) correct some keys with numeric codes
 For some unknown reason, there were a few keys (15) which had
 numbers in their key. I thought this was taken care of 
 in (construct-SLmwtot3), and except for these keys it was.
 Changes were from file 'modify-SLmwtot3-6a1.txt'

** (modify-SLmwtot3-6a2) correct 'vira-' keys to 'vIra-'
The noun 'vIra' is misspelled, along with 157 of its compounds,
as 'vira'. This error is corrected.

** (modify-SLmwtot3-6b1) lexcodes PRON, CARD, ORD
 The lexical specification of pronouns and numbers is made variously
 in MW. This changes to these standard forms for some common pronouns
 and numbers.  
 Only cardinals 1-19 are present.

2243 changes:
 (modify-SLmwtot3-6b3-PFX)
 Example: '(%{akzRayA-}) <LEX=adj>' -> '<LEX=adj> <PFX=%{akzRayA-}>'
  Any of the lex codes m,f,n,adj,I,CARD (PRON and ORD have none) are included.
  The prefix has the form %{...-}, i.e., the bracketed item ends in -, and
  just includes letters or dashes.
 This kind of info preceding the lexical specification appears to be purely
  informational in the sense that it has no bearing on how to interpret the
  lexical specification.

887 changes:
 (modify-SLmwtot3-6b3-PFX1)
 Just like above, but prefix is preceded and followed by '-', whereas
 above prefix is only followed by '-'.

3530 changes:
 (modify-SLmwtot3-6b3-PFX2)
 Just like above, but prefix can be anything. Note, these changes are
 not applied to verbs, and apply only at the beginning of a data record
 (they do not apply following semicolons, as in this situation some
 prefixes specify that the word to which the clause applies is
 some modification of the key for the record.

3309 changes:
 (modify-SLmwtot3-mfn "; (%{A}) <LEX=f>"  ?a  "; <LEX=f>")
14 changes:
 (modify-SLmwtot3-mfn "; (%{A}) <LEX=f>"  ?A  "; <LEX=f>")
2 changes:
 (modify-SLmwtot3-mfn "<LEX=mfn>"  nil "<LEX=adj>")

 14937 changes:
 (modify-SLmwtot3-reformat "\\([a-zA-Z0-9]+\\)#\\1" "\\1")
  Example: gr#great -> great  
  These are how abbreviations used by MW were treated by the Cologne
  team:  The original abbreviation as well as the expansion are show.

** abbrev1.txt
 creation
 This file was created in directory 'mw' by the following:
 (regexp-distrib "SL-mwtot3.txt" 
  "\\W\\([ABCDEGHIJKLMNOPRSTUVY][abcdeghijklmnoprstuvy123456]+[.]\\)" 1)
 It contains 1674 records indicating 24639 possible abbreviations,
 defined as a word beginning with a capital letter (except FQWXZ) and
 followed by 1 or more lower case letters (except fqwxz) or digits 123456,
 and followed by a period. 
 Now some of these are abbreviations of English words (e.g. 'Caus.' for
 'causal'), most of which are indicated in a prefatory section of MW,
 and which appear in the file 'mw-abbrev.txt'.

* LEX-CONSTRUCT
 This details the construction of files in "inputs" directory

** MW-inputs-1
(defun construct-MW-inputs-1-all ()

 (construct-MW-inputs-1a "MW-noun.txt" nil "m?f?n?" nil "all")
 (construct-MW-inputs-1a "MW-adj.txt" " +" "adj" "ADJ" "all")
 (construct-MW-inputs-1a "MW-ind.txt" " +" "I" "IND" "all")
 (construct-MW-inputs-1b "MW-verb.txt" "V[ 0-9AP]+")
 (construct-MW-inputs-1c "MW-preverb.txt" "V[ 0-9AP]+")
)
# records in MW-noun.txt  = 116044
# records in MW-adj.txt  = 48107
# records in MW-ind.txt  = 3128
 Subtotal 135506
# records in MW-verb.txt  = 1935 
# records in MW-preverb.txt  = 6323

 TOTAL 141844 records constructed
  NOTE: Only non-prefixed verbs are in MW-verb.txt; their lexical form
   is V[ 0-9AP]+. And only prefixed verbs are in MW-preverb.txt.
  In MW-verb.txt, some roots have more than one record. There are 
  1545 different roots.

** comparison with previously gathered verbs.
From the files 'dhaatukosha.txt, d..1.txt, d..2.txt' were constructed
 dk-verb.txt (894 unprefixed verb lines)
 dk-preverb.txt (168 prefixed verb lines)
There were compared with
 mw-verb.txt (1935 unprefixed verb lines)
 mw-preverb.txt (6323 prefixed verb lines).
I was mainly curious if the 'dk' verbs formed a subset of the 'mw' verbs;
my hope is that this is nearly true - I expect there to be a few exceptions.

** 88 of the 142 verbs did appear as a key in mw-verb.
However, these have not been recognized as verbs, for one reason
or another.
Here is the MW data found:
OLD 21546   ay

NOTE: 'code 39' = a single quote "'"
      'code 56' = the numeral "8"
      'code 2291' : This is a a two-byte character, which is odd.
      Since 2291 = (+ (* 8 256) 243), the two bytes are '8' and '243'.

      abbreviated words expanded".

* the question of 'L' 
  (MWindx-test1 "L" t)
 Based on examples #19 and 22, and Apte's Practical Sanskrit Dictionary,
 it appears that 'L' represents the voice-unaspirated-cerebral consonant
 ('D' in ITRANS, 'q' in SLP1)

* Scharf's response on the question of 'L':
Dear Jim,
        Thanks for the evidence!  The instances of L you send are of two sorts:
1.      Lh = | (pipe=capital backslash) (SLP1) intervocalic alternate of Dh (HK) = Q (SLP1)
#19: krILu4 ->  mfn. playing (Soma) RV. ix , 20 , 7.
 Question: does he mean #20:
  #20: ja4Lhu ->  mfn.

2.      L = L (SLP1) " " " D (HK) = q (SLP1)
#22: naLa4 ->  m. 

Although these are intervocalic alternates in the Rgvedic dialect, they should be encoded as shown rather than identically with their alternates which whould blur the dialectal difference.  Some texts use both, one for citation and the other for a gloss.
In 30-31 the space between R and L is erroneous.
In 33 the space between ra and tho is erroneous.

EJF NOTE: the spaces are due to presence of '\363' = [d'243] = 
  'circled Sanskrit abbreviated words expanded'

* Scharf on 'L' (12-11-03)
        Correction on transliteration into SLP1: Lh -> | (pipe), else L -> L
which you confused with my phonetic explanation that the sounds these encodings represent (whatever their encoding) are Rgvedic dialectal allophonic variants of the phonetically different sounds represented by Q and q when they occur intervocalically.

RE:
I have kept your response (Lh -> Q, else L -> q) for reference in parsing CDSL into SLP1.

* occurence of Adjectives in mwtot2
 (MWtot2-test1 "mfn.") -> 46272 lines

 (MWtot2-test1 "mf(") -> 6364 lines

ncount = 49136

* hyphens
 A hyphen (the character '-') is used to make visible the
 constituents of a compound; for example
 'Asana-sTa 1717724 mfn. abiding on a seat , sitting'.

* hyphens and sandhi
 Sandhi may be applicable between the components of a compound.
 'anu' + 'ita' -> 'anvita'.  In MW, this is 'anv-ita',
 so the original form is not visible, but the correct final spelling
 is obtained by omitting the hyphen.

* double hyphens
 There are 1275 instances of words spelled with a double hyphens ('--').
 In general, these seem to occur when the following component is
 somehow modified in its presence in the current compound; for instance:
  A-SA--nvita 1686924  (%{AzA7nvita}) mfn. having hope.
 This appears in MW book with a 'circle' preceding 'nvita', and is
 followed by a parenthetical spelling of the final form. In this case
 'nvita' stands for the form 'anvita', which appears as
   'anv-ita  mfn. gone along with', from the prefixed verb "anu i".

* ending hyphens
 In 46 cases, an entry in mwindx.tot ends in a hyphen. For instance,
  'sarvADika- 14968435 mfn. superior to everything Bha1m.'
 In the book, (p. 1188) I see nothing special about this.
 Possibly, this is just a processing artifact which should be ignored.

* Four 'lines' of words in MW
  In intro, p. xiv:
  "... there are four mutually correlated lines of Sanskrit
and there are four mutually correlated lines of Sanskrit words
in this dictionary:
(1) a main line in Nagari type, with equivalents in Indo-Italic type;
 this constitutes the principal series all of Sanskrit words to which
the eye must first turn on consulting the dictionary. It comprises
all the roots of the language, both genuine and artificial (the genuine
being in large Nagari type), as well as many leading words, in small 
Nagari type, and many isolated words ( also in small Nagari type),
some of which have their etymologies given in parentheses, while others
have their derivation indicated by hyphens.
(2) a subordinate line (under the Nagari)in thick Indo-Romanic type.
this line serves two purposes:
(a)for exhibiting clearly to the eyein regular sequence under every root
the continuous series of derivative words which grow out  of each root.
(b)for exhibiting those series of cognate words which, to promote facility
of reference, are placed under certain leading words (in small Nagari)
rather than under the roots themselves.
(3) a branch line, also in thick Indo-Romanic type,branching off from
either the first or the second lines with the object of grouping
compound words under one head;
(4) a branch line in Indo-Italic type, branching off from leading
compounds with the object of grouping together the compounds of
 those compounds.

* commas and semicolons
MW: "As to the separation of meanings it must be noted that mere amplifications
of preceding meanings are separated by commas,whereas those which do not
clearly run into each other are divided by semicolons. A comma therefore
must always be taken as marking separate shades of meaning, except in
parenthetical observations. "

* parenthetical comments
MW: "All remarks upon meanings and all descriptive and explanatory
statements are given between parentheses, all remarks within remarks
and comparisons with other languages are given between brackets."

* multiple entries
 Sometimes, there are two (non-contiguous) entries with identical final
 forms. The 'aja' example suggests several generalizations:
 - the first character of the definition is non-blank ('1' or '2').
   For most words, this first character is blank
 - the hyphenation of the entries differs, reflecting the
   different etymologies.

* brace-embedded Sanskrit
 There are many instances of transliterated Sanskrit embedded in braces, e.g.
  '%{pUrvA7SADha}'. The transliteration scheme is the same as that of the
 entries, i.e., what I refer to as 'MWHK'.  However, note that
 embedded spaces are represented by '@', for instance
 {ajananir@astu@tasya}

* semicolons
  Within an entry, semicolon separate major usages.
  Sometimes, the subentries only indicate semantic variations:
anv-aya
 m. ( %{i} see %{anv-i}) , following , succession [46,2] ; connection , association , being linked to or concerned with ; the natural order or connection of words in a sentence , syntax , construing ; logical connection of words ; logical connection of cause and effect , or proposition and conclusion ; drift , tenor , purport ; descendants , race , lineage , family.

  Sometimes, the subentries refer to additional words that could be
  considered to deserve separate entries. For instance,
anvaYc
 %{aN} , %{UcI} and %{UcI} , %{ak} ( %{aYc}) , following the direction of another , going after , following ; lying lengthwise ; (%{anUci}) loc. ind. in the rear , behind ; (%{ak}) ind. afterwards ; behind (with acc.)

  For verbs, sometimes subentries identify different conjugational forms:
apa-vic
 cl. 7. %{-vinakti} (impf. %{apAvinak}) to single out from , select AV. S3Br. ; cl. 3. %{-vevekti} id. Kaus3. 


* semicolon distribution (2-22-04)
The function call (MW-clause-distrib (SLtot3-buffer)) gave 
information on the number of semicolons per record.
About 75% of the records have no semicolons, meaning they have only
one major 'sense'.
At the other extreme, the word 'su-ganDa' showed 51 semicolons. An
examination suggests that in fact many additional compounds may be
embedded in this definition; e.g., 'su-ganDa-tA' (fragrance). This will
have to be sorted out later.

* 'embedded' entries.
 Based on example of 'ajagAva', a given entry may contain what could be
 represented as other entries.  e.g., 'ajagAva' 

** example 'ajagAva'
ajagAva
 m. N. of a snake demon Ta1n2d2yaBr. ; cf. %{AjagAva} ; (%{am}) n. S3iva's bow L. ; N. of the sacrificial vessel also called %{ajakAva4} (q.v.) A1pS3r.

* 'circled Sanskrit word'
 The character with decimal Ascii code 243 is in the Buontes MW file
 representing the 'circle' symbol in MW. This character has no standard
 print representation. In the example, I have made it appear [d'243].
 In the MW dictionary, we see a line on p. 1307 which might be represented as
  "hrIvela and [circle]laka n. id. L." 
 This line follows the entry 'hrIvera'.
 Here is how it is represented in the Buontes file:
hrIvera
 n. a kind of Andropogon (accord. to some , Pavonia Odorata) Sus3r. 
hrIvela
 and n. id. L. [1307,3]
hrIve[d'243]laka
 n. id. L. [1307,3] 

Note: In automatic translation, I just skip this character.

* '@' sign.
 The '@' sign is used to represent a space character in %{} embedded
 Sanskrit.
** @ sign in index words
 From the example, one guesses that '@' might represent a hyphen which
 happened to be printed at the end of a line in the book. 
 Note that 'ajAjIva' shows with an end-of-line hyphen in the book, but
 has no '@' between 'A' and 'j'. 

ajAjIva
 m. `" who lives by goats , a goat-herd.
ajA@taulvali
 m. N. of a Muni who lived on the milk of goats (an example of compounds in which the middle term is left out , g. %{zAkapArthivA7di} q.v.).

** @ sign in %{} embedded text
 The '@' sign in embedded text appears to represent a space. For instance,
a-janani
 f. (generally used in cursing) , nonbirth , cessation of existence ; %{ajananir@astu@tasya} , `" may he cease to exist! "' Pan5cat. ; cf. Pa1n2. 3-3 , 112. 

* lexical categories 02-12-04
** original MW coding
 (p.6 of Cologne Digitial Sanskrit Protocol):
 Grammar: The indication of gender (m., f., n.) of nouns and in the
  case of verbs conjugation has been marked as far as possible by 
  a preceding [d'250] 
 In Mwtot2, the [d'250] codes are absent, so
 an indirect deterination of lexical category must be attempted,
 using a search by regular expressions.
** nouns
  " m.": masculine 
  " f.": feminine
  " n.": neuter
  " mfn.": adjectives, with no special feminine form
  " mf(%{...})n.": adjectives, with some peculiarity in feminine form
  The following listing shows the result of a search for all nominal
  forms, and the frequency of occurrence.
  There are more forms than dictionary entries, since different 
  entry 'clauses' may have different lexical forms. Also, sometimes
  one of these forms may occur within a parenthetical comment.
  Also, some of these forms (e.g., "ff.") are not lexical forms.
  Over 96% of the forms are 'simple' concatenations of gender symbols;
  except for " ff.", these almost surely represent lexical specifications.
(SLtot3-lexical-find 's)
REGEXP-gen=\( \([mfn]\(([^)]*) ?\)?\)+[.]\)

** indeclineables
 These are indicated by the abbreviation " ind.".
(progn
(fol-msg (format "%s\n" (current-time-string)))
(SLtot3-lexical-find 'i)
(fol-msg (format "%s\n" (current-time-string)))
)
Fri Feb 20 10:19:49 2004
REGEXP-gen= ind[.]
ncount = 6006
' ind.'  6006
Fri Feb 20 10:19:50 2004


** verbs indicated by 'cl.'
 These are in general indicated by " cl. n p", where n is 
 a number from 1 to 10, and p is 'P' or 'A1'.
 Here are how the variations on this theme appear:
REGEXP-gen=\( cl[.][0-9IilrPA. ]+[.]\| cl[.] *[0-9]+ \| cl[.]\)

** prefixed verbs
 Many prefixed verbs appear as entries without the 'cl.' specification.
 In the MW text, a root sign often appears; in the original the
 extended ASCII character [d'251] is used to tag the root. However,
 this is absent in mwtot2. Thus, other techniques must be used.
 Some examples show the range of possibilities:

apa-vI
 %{-veti} , to turn away from , be unfavourable to RV. v , 61 , 18 and x , 43 , 2. 
amb
 cl. 1. P. %{ambati} , to go L.: cl. 1. A1. %{ambate} , to sound L. 
aww
 cl. 1. A1. %{awwate} , %{Anawwe} , %{awwitum} , to exceed L. ; to kill L.: cl. 10. P. %{awwayati} , to contemn L. ; to lessen , diminish L. 
ati-pra-RaS
 (2. %{naS}) , to be entirely deprived of (acc.) S3Br. 
ati-pra-RI
 ( %{ni}) , to lead by or beyond La1t2y. A1s3vS3r. 
ati-pra-Rud
 ( %{nud}) , to press or incite very strongly. 
ati-pra-pad
 Caus. %{-pAdayati} , to help to pass into the other world (Comm.) MBh. iv , 1717. 
ati-pra-Sru
 cl. 5. A1. %{-SfRve} (Ved. 3. sg.) , to become known or famous more than others RV. x , II , 7. 
ati-pra-sad
 P. %{-sidati} , to be come completely cheerful. 
ati-pra-sf
 Intens. %{-sarsfte} , to outstrip , surpass RV. ii , 25 , 1. 
ati-Sliz
 to fasten or tie over. 
ati-sf
 Caus. to cause to pass through: Pass. %{-sAryate} ; to be purged Sus3r. 
ati-hA
 2. to jump over RV. &c. ; to pass jumping from one place to another.
